## Chapter: Applications of Integration

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Applications of Integration, a pivotal component of our book, "Mastering Calculus: A Comprehensive Guide to AP®︎/College Calculus BC". This chapter is designed to provide you with a comprehensive understanding of how integration, a fundamental concept in calculus, can be applied in various contexts and fields.

Integration, as you may recall from previous chapters, is a mathematical process that is essentially the reverse of differentiation. It is used to calculate areas, volumes, central points, and many other useful things. But it is easier to start with finding the area under the curve of a function like $f(x)$ over a certain interval on the x-axis.

In this chapter, we will delve into the practical applications of integration. We will explore how integration is used in physics to determine quantities such as displacement, velocity, and acceleration. In the field of engineering, integration is used to calculate the center of mass and the moment of inertia. Economists use integration to calculate consumer and producer surplus, among other things.

We will also explore how integration is used in the field of statistics to calculate probabilities and expected values. In the realm of computer science, integration plays a key role in algorithms related to graphics and simulations.

This chapter will not only provide you with the theoretical knowledge of these applications but also equip you with the skills to solve real-world problems using integration. By the end of this chapter, you will have a deeper understanding of why integration is a powerful tool in mathematics and its numerous applications in various fields.

Remember, calculus is not just about solving mathematical problems; it's about understanding how different components of our world interact and change. So, let's dive into the fascinating world of integration applications!

### Section: Average Value of a Function

In the previous sections, we have explored various applications of integration, from calculating areas and volumes to determining quantities in physics and economics. Now, we will turn our attention to another important application of integration: finding the average value of a function on an interval.

#### Subsection: Finding the average value of a function on an interval

The concept of an average is something we are all familiar with. In everyday life, we often calculate averages to get a sense of the "typical" or "central" value in a set of data. In calculus, we extend this concept to functions. The average value of a function over an interval gives us a single number that represents the "typical" value of the function on that interval.

The average value of a function $f(x)$ on the interval $[a, b]$ is defined as:

$$
f_{avg} = \frac{1}{b - a} \int_{a}^{b} f(x) \, dx
$$

This formula might look a bit intimidating at first, but it's actually quite intuitive. The integral $\int_{a}^{b} f(x) \, dx$ gives us the total value of the function on the interval $[a, b]$. We then divide this total by the length of the interval $(b - a)$ to find the average.

Let's illustrate this with an example.

**Example:** Find the average value of the function $f(x) = x^2$ on the interval $[1, 3]$.

First, we calculate the integral of $f(x)$ from $1$ to $3$:

$$
\int_{1}^{3} x^2 \, dx = \left[ \frac{1}{3}x^3 \right]_1^3 = \frac{1}{3}(27 - 1) = \frac{26}{3}
$$

Next, we divide this by the length of the interval $(3 - 1)$ to find the average:

$$
f_{avg} = \frac{1}{3 - 1} \cdot \frac{26}{3} = \frac{13}{3}
$$

So, the average value of the function $f(x) = x^2$ on the interval $[1, 3]$ is $\frac{13}{3}$.

In the next section, we will explore how to use this concept to solve more complex problems. Stay tuned!

### Section: Applications of the Average Value of a Function

In the previous section, we learned how to calculate the average value of a function over an interval. Now, let's explore some applications of this concept. The average value of a function can be used in various fields such as physics, economics, and engineering to solve real-world problems.

#### Subsection: Applications in Physics

In physics, the average value of a function is often used to calculate average velocities, average temperatures, or average densities over a certain period or region.

**Example:** Suppose a particle moves along a line according to the function $s(t) = t^3 - 3t^2 + 2t$, where $s$ is the position of the particle at time $t$. Find the average velocity of the particle over the interval $[1, 4]$.

The velocity function $v(t)$ is the derivative of the position function $s(t)$, so $v(t) = 3t^2 - 6t + 2$. The average velocity $v_{avg}$ over the interval $[1, 4]$ is then given by:

$$
v_{avg} = \frac{1}{4 - 1} \int_{1}^{4} (3t^2 - 6t + 2) \, dt
$$

Calculating the integral and dividing by the length of the interval, we find that the average velocity of the particle over the interval $[1, 4]$ is $v_{avg} = 2$.

#### Subsection: Applications in Economics

In economics, the average value of a function can be used to calculate average costs, average revenues, or average profits over a certain period.

**Example:** Suppose a company's cost function over a week is given by $C(x) = 100x^2 - 500x + 2500$, where $x$ is the number of units produced and $C(x)$ is the cost in dollars. Find the average cost per unit over the week if the company produces between 10 and 20 units.

The average cost $C_{avg}$ is given by:

$$
C_{avg} = \frac{1}{20 - 10} \int_{10}^{20} (100x^2 - 500x + 2500) \, dx
$$

Calculating the integral and dividing by the number of units produced, we find that the average cost per unit over the week is $C_{avg} = 1500$ dollars.

These examples illustrate how the concept of the average value of a function can be applied in various fields. In the next section, we will explore more advanced applications of integration. Stay tuned!

### Section: Connecting Position, Velocity, and Acceleration

In this section, we will explore the connection between position, velocity, and acceleration using the concepts of differentiation and integration. These concepts are fundamental in physics, particularly in the study of motion.

#### Subsection: Connecting position, velocity, and acceleration functions using integrals

The position, velocity, and acceleration of an object in motion are all related through calculus. If we have a function that describes the position of an object, we can find the velocity and acceleration of the object by using derivatives. Conversely, if we have a function that describes the acceleration of an object, we can find the velocity and position by using integrals.

Let's consider a particle moving along a line. The position of the particle at any time $t$ is given by a function $s(t)$. The velocity of the particle at any time $t$ is the derivative of the position function, $v(t) = s'(t)$. The acceleration of the particle at any time $t$ is the derivative of the velocity function, $a(t) = v'(t)$, which is also the second derivative of the position function, $a(t) = s''(t)$.

Conversely, if we know the acceleration function $a(t)$, we can find the velocity function by integrating the acceleration function, $v(t) = \int a(t) \, dt$, and we can find the position function by integrating the velocity function, $s(t) = \int v(t) \, dt$.

**Example:** Suppose a particle moves along a line with acceleration given by the function $a(t) = 6t - 4$. Find the velocity and position functions of the particle.

The velocity function $v(t)$ is the integral of the acceleration function $a(t)$, so $v(t) = \int (6t - 4) \, dt = 3t^2 - 4t + C$, where $C$ is the constant of integration.

The position function $s(t)$ is the integral of the velocity function $v(t)$, so $s(t) = \int (3t^2 - 4t + C) \, dt = t^3 - 2t^2 + Ct + D$, where $D$ is another constant of integration.

The constants $C$ and $D$ can be determined if we know the initial velocity and position of the particle.

This example illustrates how we can use integrals to connect acceleration, velocity, and position functions. In the next section, we will explore more applications of integration in physics.

#### Subsection: Applications of integrals in motion problems

Integrals are not only useful in finding the position and velocity of an object given its acceleration, but they also play a crucial role in solving various motion problems. Let's explore some of these applications.

**Example 1:** Suppose a car starts from rest and accelerates at a rate of $a(t) = 3t^2$ m/s². What is the car's velocity after 5 seconds?

In this case, we need to find the velocity function $v(t)$ by integrating the acceleration function $a(t)$. So, we have $v(t) = \int a(t) \, dt = \int 3t^2 \, dt = t^3 + C$. Since the car starts from rest, we know that $v(0) = 0$, so $C = 0$. Therefore, the velocity function is $v(t) = t^3$.

To find the car's velocity after 5 seconds, we simply substitute $t = 5$ into the velocity function: $v(5) = 5^3 = 125$ m/s.

**Example 2:** A particle moves along a line with its velocity given by $v(t) = 2t^3 - 3t^2 + 2$. Find the displacement of the particle between $t = 1$ and $t = 3$.

The displacement of the particle is given by the integral of the velocity function over the interval from $t = 1$ to $t = 3$. So, we have $s = \int_{1}^{3} v(t) \, dt = \int_{1}^{3} (2t^3 - 3t^2 + 2) \, dt$.

Evaluating this integral gives $s = [0.5t^4 - t^3 + 2t]_{1}^{3} = (0.5*3^4 - 3^3 + 2*3) - (0.5*1^4 - 1^3 + 2*1) = 9$ m.

So, the displacement of the particle between $t = 1$ and $t = 3$ is 9 meters.

These examples illustrate how integrals can be used to solve motion problems involving position, velocity, and acceleration. By understanding these concepts and mastering the techniques of integration, you will be well-equipped to tackle a wide range of problems in physics and engineering.

#### Subsection: Using accumulation functions and definite integrals in applied contexts

In the previous section, we explored how integrals can be used to solve motion problems. Now, let's delve deeper into the applied contexts of accumulation functions and definite integrals. Accumulation functions are a special type of function that represent the total accumulation of a quantity over an interval. They are often used in physics, economics, and other fields to model the accumulation of quantities such as distance, mass, or wealth over time.

**Example 3:** Suppose a factory produces widgets at a rate of $r(t) = 5t^2$ widgets per hour, where $t$ is the number of hours since the start of the workday. How many widgets does the factory produce between 2 and 5 hours into the workday?

In this case, we need to find the total number of widgets produced over the interval from $t = 2$ to $t = 5$. This is given by the definite integral of the rate function over this interval. So, we have $N = \int_{2}^{5} r(t) \, dt = \int_{2}^{5} 5t^2 \, dt$.

Evaluating this integral gives $N = [5/3t^3]_{2}^{5} = (5/3*5^3) - (5/3*2^3) = 155$ widgets.

So, the factory produces 155 widgets between 2 and 5 hours into the workday.

**Example 4:** A tank is being filled with water at a rate of $r(t) = 10t$ liters per minute, where $t$ is the number of minutes since the tank started filling. What is the total volume of water in the tank after 10 minutes?

The total volume of water in the tank is given by the integral of the rate function over the interval from $t = 0$ to $t = 10$. So, we have $V = \int_{0}^{10} r(t) \, dt = \int_{0}^{10} 10t \, dt$.

Evaluating this integral gives $V = [5t^2]_{0}^{10} = 5*10^2 = 500$ liters.

So, the total volume of water in the tank after 10 minutes is 500 liters.

These examples illustrate how accumulation functions and definite integrals can be used in applied contexts to model the accumulation of quantities over time. By understanding these concepts and mastering the techniques of integration, you will be well-equipped to solve a wide range of problems in various fields.

#### Subsection: Real-world applications of accumulation functions and definite integrals

Accumulation functions and definite integrals are not just abstract mathematical concepts. They have numerous real-world applications in various fields such as physics, economics, engineering, and environmental science. Let's explore a few more examples to illustrate their practical utility.

**Example 5:** Consider a car that accelerates from rest at a rate of $a(t) = 3t$ meters per second squared, where $t$ is the time in seconds. What is the car's velocity after 5 seconds?

The velocity of the car is the integral of the acceleration function over the interval from $t = 0$ to $t = 5$. So, we have $v = \int_{0}^{5} a(t) \, dt = \int_{0}^{5} 3t \, dt$.

Evaluating this integral gives $v = [1.5t^2]_{0}^{5} = 1.5*5^2 = 37.5$ meters per second.

So, the car's velocity after 5 seconds is 37.5 meters per second.

**Example 6:** An investment account earns interest at a rate of $r(t) = 0.05t$ dollars per year, where $t$ is the number of years since the investment was made. How much interest does the account earn over the first 10 years?

The total interest earned over the first 10 years is given by the integral of the rate function over the interval from $t = 0$ to $t = 10$. So, we have $I = \int_{0}^{10} r(t) \, dt = \int_{0}^{10} 0.05t \, dt$.

Evaluating this integral gives $I = [0.025t^2]_{0}^{10} = 0.025*10^2 = 25$ dollars.

So, the account earns 25 dollars in interest over the first 10 years.

These examples demonstrate how accumulation functions and definite integrals can be used to model real-world scenarios. By understanding these mathematical concepts, you can solve complex problems in various fields. In the next section, we will explore more advanced applications of integration.

#### Subsection: Finding the area between curves expressed as functions of x

In the previous sections, we have learned how to use integration to find the area under a curve. Now, we will extend this concept to find the area between two curves. This is a common problem in calculus and has many practical applications.

Consider two functions $f(x)$ and $g(x)$ where $f(x) \geq g(x)$ for all $x$ in the interval $[a, b]$. The area between these two curves from $x = a$ to $x = b$ is given by the definite integral:

$$
A = \int_{a}^{b} [f(x) - g(x)] \, dx
$$

This formula is derived from the concept that the area under the curve $f(x)$ from $x = a$ to $x = b$ is $\int_{a}^{b} f(x) \, dx$ and the area under the curve $g(x)$ from $x = a$ to $x = b$ is $\int_{a}^{b} g(x) \, dx$. The difference between these two areas gives the area between the curves.

Let's illustrate this concept with an example.

**Example 7:** Find the area between the curves $y = x^2$ and $y = x$ from $x = 0$ to $x = 1$.

First, we need to determine which function is above the other in the given interval. In this case, $x^2 \leq x$ for all $x$ in $[0, 1]$. So, we have $f(x) = x$ and $g(x) = x^2$.

The area between the curves is given by:

$$
A = \int_{0}^{1} [f(x) - g(x)] \, dx = \int_{0}^{1} [x - x^2] \, dx
$$

Evaluating this integral gives:

$$
A = \left[\frac{1}{2}x^2 - \frac{1}{3}x^3\right]_{0}^{1} = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{3} = \frac{1}{6}
$$

So, the area between the curves $y = x^2$ and $y = x$ from $x = 0$ to $x = 1$ is $\frac{1}{6}$ square units.

This method can be used to find the area between any two curves expressed as functions of $x$. In the next subsection, we will explore how to find the area between curves expressed as functions of $y$.

#### Subsection: Finding the area between curves expressed as functions of y

In the previous subsection, we learned how to find the area between two curves expressed as functions of $x$. Now, we will extend this concept to find the area between two curves expressed as functions of $y$.

Consider two functions $h(y)$ and $k(y)$ where $h(y) \geq k(y)$ for all $y$ in the interval $[c, d]$. The area between these two curves from $y = c$ to $y = d$ is given by the definite integral:

$$
A = \int_{c}^{d} [h(y) - k(y)] \, dy
$$

This formula is derived from the concept that the area under the curve $h(y)$ from $y = c$ to $y = d$ is $\int_{c}^{d} h(y) \, dy$ and the area under the curve $k(y)$ from $y = c$ to $y = d$ is $\int_{c}^{d} k(y) \, dy$. The difference between these two areas gives the area between the curves.

Let's illustrate this concept with an example.

**Example 8:** Find the area between the curves $x = y^2$ and $x = y$ from $y = 0$ to $y = 1$.

First, we need to determine which function is to the right of the other in the given interval. In this case, $y^2 \leq y$ for all $y$ in $[0, 1]$. So, we have $h(y) = y$ and $k(y) = y^2$.

The area between the curves is given by:

$$
A = \int_{0}^{1} [h(y) - k(y)] \, dy = \int_{0}^{1} [y - y^2] \, dy
$$

Evaluating this integral gives:

$$
A = \left[\frac{1}{2}y^2 - \frac{1}{3}y^3\right]_{0}^{1} = \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{3} = \frac{1}{6}
$$

So, the area between the curves $x = y^2$ and $x = y$ from $y = 0$ to $y = 1$ is $\frac{1}{6}$ square units.

This method can be used to find the area between any two curves expressed as functions of $y$. In the next sections, we will explore more applications of integration.

### Section: Volumes with Cross Sections

In the previous sections, we have explored the concept of integration to find the area under a curve and between two curves. Now, we will extend this concept to find the volume of a solid with known cross sections.

#### Subsection: Volumes with cross sections: squares and rectangles

Consider a solid whose base is the region in the xy-plane bounded by the curves $y = f(x)$ and $y = g(x)$ for $a \leq x \leq b$ and whose cross sections perpendicular to the x-axis are squares or rectangles.

##### Volumes with Square Cross Sections

If the cross sections are squares, then the area of each cross section is given by $A(x) = [f(x) - g(x)]^2$. The volume of the solid is then given by the definite integral:

$$
V = \int_{a}^{b} A(x) \, dx = \int_{a}^{b} [f(x) - g(x)]^2 \, dx
$$

##### Volumes with Rectangular Cross Sections

If the cross sections are rectangles with height $h(x)$, then the area of each cross section is given by $A(x) = h(x) [f(x) - g(x)]$. The volume of the solid is then given by the definite integral:

$$
V = \int_{a}^{b} A(x) \, dx = \int_{a}^{b} h(x) [f(x) - g(x)] \, dx
$$

Let's illustrate these concepts with examples.

**Example 9:** Find the volume of the solid whose base is the region in the xy-plane bounded by the curves $y = x^2$ and $y = x$ for $0 \leq x \leq 1$ and whose cross sections perpendicular to the x-axis are squares.

In this case, we have $f(x) = x$ and $g(x) = x^2$. The area of each cross section is $A(x) = [f(x) - g(x)]^2 = [x - x^2]^2$. The volume of the solid is then given by:

$$
V = \int_{0}^{1} A(x) \, dx = \int_{0}^{1} [x - x^2]^2 \, dx
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the volume of the solid.

**Example 10:** Find the volume of the solid whose base is the region in the xy-plane bounded by the curves $y = x^2$ and $y = x$ for $0 \leq x \leq 1$ and whose cross sections perpendicular to the x-axis are rectangles with height $h(x) = x$.

In this case, the area of each cross section is $A(x) = h(x) [f(x) - g(x)] = x [x - x^2]$. The volume of the solid is then given by:

$$
V = \int_{0}^{1} A(x) \, dx = \int_{0}^{1} x [x - x^2] \, dx
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the volume of the solid.

These methods can be used to find the volume of a solid with known cross sections. In the next sections, we will explore more applications of integration.

#### Volumes with Triangular Cross Sections

If the cross sections are triangles, then the area of each cross section is given by $A(x) = \frac{1}{2} [f(x) - g(x)]^2$. The volume of the solid is then given by the definite integral:

$$
V = \int_{a}^{b} A(x) \, dx = \int_{a}^{b} \frac{1}{2} [f(x) - g(x)]^2 \, dx
$$

**Example 11:** Find the volume of the solid whose base is the region in the xy-plane bounded by the curves $y = x^2$ and $y = x$ for $0 \leq x \leq 1$ and whose cross sections perpendicular to the x-axis are triangles.

In this case, we have $f(x) = x$ and $g(x) = x^2$. The area of each cross section is $A(x) = \frac{1}{2} [f(x) - g(x)]^2 = \frac{1}{2} [x - x^2]^2$. The volume of the solid is then given by:

$$
V = \int_{0}^{1} A(x) \, dx = \int_{0}^{1} \frac{1}{2} [x - x^2]^2 \, dx
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the volume of the solid.

#### Volumes with Semicircular Cross Sections

If the cross sections are semicircles, then the area of each cross section is given by $A(x) = \frac{1}{2} \pi [f(x) - g(x)]^2$. The volume of the solid is then given by the definite integral:

$$
V = \int_{a}^{b} A(x) \, dx = \int_{a}^{b} \frac{1}{2} \pi [f(x) - g(x)]^2 \, dx
$$

**Example 12:** Find the volume of the solid whose base is the region in the xy-plane bounded by the curves $y = x^2$ and $y = x$ for $0 \leq x \leq 1$ and whose cross sections perpendicular to the x-axis are semicircles.

In this case, we have $f(x) = x$ and $g(x) = x^2$. The area of each cross section is $A(x) = \frac{1}{2} \pi [f(x) - g(x)]^2 = \frac{1}{2} \pi [x - x^2]^2$. The volume of the solid is then given by:

$$
V = \int_{0}^{1} A(x) \, dx = \int_{0}^{1} \frac{1}{2} \pi [x - x^2]^2 \, dx
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the volume of the solid.

### Section: Volume with Disc and Washer Methods

The disc and washer methods are two powerful techniques for finding the volume of a solid of revolution. A solid of revolution is a three-dimensional figure that is obtained by rotating a two-dimensional shape (or a region of the plane) around a line.

#### Volume with disc method: revolving around x- or y-axis

The disc method is used when the slice that we revolve to generate the solid is perpendicular to the axis of revolution.

##### Disc Method around the x-axis

If we revolve a region bounded by $y = f(x)$, the x-axis, and the vertical lines $x = a$ and $x = b$ around the x-axis, the resulting solid has volume:

$$
V = \pi \int_{a}^{b} [f(x)]^2 \, dx
$$

The integrand, $[f(x)]^2$, represents the area of a disc at position x. The integral then sums these infinitesimally thin discs from $x = a$ to $x = b$ to find the total volume.

**Example 13:** Find the volume of the solid obtained by revolving the region bounded by $y = x^2$ and the x-axis, for $0 \leq x \leq 1$, around the x-axis.

In this case, we have $f(x) = x^2$. The volume of the solid is then given by:

$$
V = \pi \int_{0}^{1} [f(x)]^2 \, dx = \pi \int_{0}^{1} (x^2)^2 \, dx = \pi \int_{0}^{1} x^4 \, dx
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the volume of the solid.

##### Disc Method around the y-axis

If we revolve a region bounded by $x = g(y)$, the y-axis, and the horizontal lines $y = c$ and $y = d$ around the y-axis, the resulting solid has volume:

$$
V = \pi \int_{c}^{d} [g(y)]^2 \, dy
$$

The integrand, $[g(y)]^2$, represents the area of a disc at position y. The integral then sums these infinitesimally thin discs from $y = c$ to $y = d$ to find the total volume.

**Example 14:** Find the volume of the solid obtained by revolving the region bounded by $x = \sqrt{y}$ and the y-axis, for $0 \leq y \leq 1$, around the y-axis.

In this case, we have $g(y) = \sqrt{y}$. The volume of the solid is then given by:

$$
V = \pi \int_{0}^{1} [g(y)]^2 \, dy = \pi \int_{0}^{1} (\sqrt{y})^2 \, dy = \pi \int_{0}^{1} y \, dy
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the volume of the solid.

#### Volume with washer method: revolving around x- or y-axis

The washer method is a slight modification of the disc method and is used when the slice that we revolve to generate the solid has a hole in it, i.e., when the region we are revolving is not adjacent to the axis of revolution.

##### Washer Method around the x-axis

If we revolve a region bounded by $y = f(x)$, $y = g(x)$, and the vertical lines $x = a$ and $x = b$ around the x-axis, the resulting solid has volume:

$$
V = \pi \int_{a}^{b} [f(x)]^2 - [g(x)]^2 \, dx
$$

The integrand, $[f(x)]^2 - [g(x)]^2$, represents the area of a washer at position x. The integral then sums these infinitesimally thin washers from $x = a$ to $x = b$ to find the total volume.

**Example 15:** Find the volume of the solid obtained by revolving the region bounded by $y = x^2$, $y = x$, and the x-axis, for $0 \leq x \leq 1$, around the x-axis.

In this case, we have $f(x) = x$ and $g(x) = x^2$. The volume of the solid is then given by:

$$
V = \pi \int_{0}^{1} [f(x)]^2 - [g(x)]^2 \, dx = \pi \int_{0}^{1} (x)^2 - (x^2)^2 \, dx = \pi \int_{0}^{1} x^2 - x^4 \, dx
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the volume of the solid.

##### Washer Method around the y-axis

If we revolve a region bounded by $x = h(y)$, $x = k(y)$, and the horizontal lines $y = c$ and $y = d$ around the y-axis, the resulting solid has volume:

$$
V = \pi \int_{c}^{d} [h(y)]^2 - [k(y)]^2 \, dy
$$

The integrand, $[h(y)]^2 - [k(y)]^2$, represents the area of a washer at position y. The integral then sums these infinitesimally thin washers from $y = c$ to $y = d$ to find the total volume.

**Example 16:** Find the volume of the solid obtained by revolving the region bounded by $x = y^2$, $x = y$, and the y-axis, for $0 \leq y \leq 1$, around the y-axis.

In this case, we have $h(y) = y$ and $k(y) = y^2$. The volume of the solid is then given by:

$$
V = \pi \int_{0}^{1} [h(y)]^2 - [k(y)]^2 \, dy = \pi \int_{0}^{1} (y)^2 - (y^2)^2 \, dy = \pi \int_{0}^{1} y^2 - y^4 \, dy
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the volume of the solid.

### Section: Arc Length and Distance Traveled

In calculus, we often encounter problems where we need to find the length of a curve or the distance traveled by an object. The concept of integration provides us with the tools to solve these problems.

#### Subsection: The Arc Length of a Smooth, Planar Curve

The arc length of a curve is the distance along the curve from one point to another. For a smooth, planar curve defined by a function $y = f(x)$, between $x = a$ and $x = b$, the arc length $L$ can be found using the formula:

$$
L = \int_{a}^{b} \sqrt{1 + [f'(x)]^2} \, dx
$$

Here, $f'(x)$ is the derivative of the function $f(x)$, and the square root expression represents the length of an infinitesimally small segment of the curve. The integral then sums these infinitesimal lengths from $x = a$ to $x = b$ to find the total arc length.

**Example 17:** Find the arc length of the curve defined by $y = x^2$, for $0 \leq x \leq 1$.

In this case, we have $f(x) = x^2$, so $f'(x) = 2x$. The arc length of the curve is then given by:

$$
L = \int_{0}^{1} \sqrt{1 + [f'(x)]^2} \, dx = \int_{0}^{1} \sqrt{1 + (2x)^2} \, dx
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the arc length of the curve.

#### Subsection: Distance Traveled

The distance traveled by an object moving along a straight line is the absolute value of the integral of its velocity function $v(t)$, from $t = a$ to $t = b$. This is given by:

$$
D = \int_{a}^{b} |v(t)| \, dt
$$

Here, $|v(t)|$ represents the speed of the object at time $t$. The integral then sums these infinitesimal distances from $t = a$ to $t = b$ to find the total distance traveled.

**Example 18:** An object moves along a straight line with velocity given by $v(t) = t^2 - t$, for $0 \leq t \leq 3$. Find the total distance traveled by the object.

In this case, we have $v(t) = t^2 - t$. The total distance traveled by the object is then given by:

$$
D = \int_{0}^{3} |v(t)| \, dt = \int_{0}^{3} |t^2 - t| \, dt
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the total distance traveled by the object.

```

### Section: Applications of Arc Length and Distance Traveled

In the previous sections, we have learned how to calculate the arc length of a curve and the distance traveled by an object. Now, let's explore some applications of these concepts in real-world scenarios.

#### Subsection: Applications of Arc Length

The concept of arc length is widely used in various fields such as physics, engineering, and computer graphics. For instance, in physics, the arc length is used to calculate the length of a trajectory of a particle moving along a curve. In engineering, it is used in the design of roads, bridges, and other structures that have curved surfaces. In computer graphics, the arc length is used to generate smooth curves and surfaces.

**Example 19:** A roller coaster track is designed as a curve defined by the function $y = x^3 - 3x^2 + 2x$, for $0 \leq x \leq 5$. Find the length of the track.

In this case, we have $f(x) = x^3 - 3x^2 + 2x$, so $f'(x) = 3x^2 - 6x + 2$. The length of the track is then given by:

$$
L = \int_{0}^{5} \sqrt{1 + [f'(x)]^2} \, dx = \int_{0}^{5} \sqrt{1 + (3x^2 - 6x + 2)^2} \, dx
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the length of the track.

#### Subsection: Applications of Distance Traveled

The concept of distance traveled is also widely used in various fields such as physics, engineering, and transportation. For instance, in physics, it is used to calculate the total distance covered by a particle moving along a straight line with a given velocity. In engineering, it is used to calculate the total distance traveled by a vehicle or a machine part. In transportation, it is used to calculate the total distance traveled by a vehicle for fuel efficiency and route planning.

**Example 20:** A car travels along a straight road with velocity given by $v(t) = 3t^2 - 2t + 1$, for $0 \leq t \leq 10$. Find the total distance traveled by the car.

In this case, we have $v(t) = 3t^2 - 2t + 1$. The total distance traveled by the car is then given by:

$$
D = \int_{0}^{10} |v(t)| \, dt = \int_{0}^{10} |3t^2 - 2t + 1| \, dt
$$

Evaluating this integral gives the total distance traveled by the car.

```

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the various applications of integration, a fundamental concept in calculus. We have delved into the practical uses of integration in solving real-world problems, demonstrating the power and versatility of calculus. We have seen how integration can be used to calculate areas under curves, volumes of solids of revolution, and lengths of curves. We have also learned how to apply integration in physics to find displacement, velocity, and acceleration, and in economics to calculate consumer and producer surplus.

The concept of integration is not just a mathematical tool, but a lens through which we can understand the world around us. From physics to economics, integration plays a crucial role in helping us model and solve complex problems. As we continue our journey in mastering calculus, it is important to remember that the power of calculus lies not just in its theoretical elegance, but also in its practical applications.

## Chapter: Parametric Equations, Polar Coordinates, and Vector-Valued Functions

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Parametric Equations, Polar Coordinates, and Vector-Valued Functions. This chapter is a crucial part of our journey through the world of Calculus BC. It introduces us to the fascinating concepts of parametric equations, polar coordinates, and vector-valued functions, which are fundamental to understanding higher-level mathematics and physics.

Parametric equations are a powerful mathematical tool that allows us to express the coordinates of points in the plane as functions of a third variable, often denoted as $t$. This method provides a more flexible way to describe geometric objects and motion in a plane. We will delve into the intricacies of parametric equations, exploring their properties, and learning how to graph and differentiate them.

Next, we will venture into the realm of polar coordinates, a two-dimensional coordinate system in which each point on a plane is determined by a distance from a reference point and an angle from a reference direction. Polar coordinates offer a unique perspective on the world of calculus, particularly when dealing with problems involving circular or rotational symmetry. We will learn how to convert between Cartesian and polar coordinates, and how to apply calculus in the polar coordinate system.

Finally, we will explore vector-valued functions, which assign a vector to each point in their domain. These functions are essential in the study of motion and forces in space, and they play a significant role in physics and engineering. We will learn how to perform calculus operations on vector-valued functions, and how to interpret and apply the results.

This chapter will provide you with a solid foundation in these topics, equipping you with the knowledge and skills necessary to tackle more complex problems in calculus. So, let's dive in and start our journey into the world of parametric equations, polar coordinates, and vector-valued functions.

### Section: Parametric Equations

#### Defining and differentiating parametric equations

Parametric equations are a set of equations that express the coordinates of points in the plane as functions of a third variable, often denoted as $t$. This third variable is called the parameter. The parameter $t$ is independent, and the coordinates $x$ and $y$ are dependent on $t$.

A parametric equation is typically written as a pair of functions where the outputs are coordinates in the plane. For example, the parametric equations $x = f(t)$ and $y = g(t)$ define a curve in the plane. Here, $f(t)$ and $g(t)$ are functions of the parameter $t$.

The parameter $t$ often represents time, especially in physics and engineering applications. In this context, the parametric equations describe the motion of an object in the plane, where $x$ and $y$ represent the position of the object at time $t$.

Let's consider an example. The parametric equations $x = t^2$ and $y = t^3 - t$ define a curve in the plane. For each value of $t$, we get a point $(x, y)$ on the curve. For example, when $t = 0$, we get the point $(0, 0)$, and when $t = 1$, we get the point $(1, 0)$.

Differentiating parametric equations involves applying the chain rule, a fundamental concept in calculus. The chain rule states that the derivative of a composite function is the derivative of the outer function times the derivative of the inner function.

To differentiate a parametric equation, we first differentiate $x = f(t)$ and $y = g(t)$ with respect to $t$ to get $x' = f'(t)$ and $y' = g'(t)$. Then, we find the derivative of $y$ with respect to $x$ by dividing $y'$ by $x'$, that is, $\frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{g'(t)}{f'(t)}$.

In the next section, we will learn how to graph parametric equations and how to use calculus to analyze the properties of the curves they define.

#### Second derivatives of parametric equations

After understanding how to differentiate parametric equations, we can now move forward to the concept of second derivatives of parametric equations. The second derivative, often denoted as $\frac{d^2y}{dx^2}$ or $y''$, is simply the derivative of the derivative. It provides information about the curvature or concavity of the graph of the function.

To find the second derivative of a parametric equation, we first need to find the first derivative $\frac{dy}{dx}$ as we did in the previous section. Then, we differentiate $\frac{dy}{dx}$ with respect to $t$ and divide by $dx/dt$ (which is $f'(t)$).

In mathematical terms, the second derivative of a parametric equation is given by:

$$
\frac{d^2y}{dx^2} = \frac{d}{dt} \left(\frac{dy}{dx}\right) \div \frac{dx}{dt} = \frac{g''(t)f'(t) - g'(t)f''(t)}{{f'(t)}^3}
$$

Let's illustrate this with an example. Consider the parametric equations $x = t^2$ and $y = t^3 - t$. The first derivatives are $x' = 2t$ and $y' = 3t^2 - 1$. The derivative of $y$ with respect to $x$ is $\frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{y'}{x'} = \frac{3t^2 - 1}{2t}$.

To find the second derivative, we first differentiate $\frac{dy}{dx}$ with respect to $t$ to get $\frac{d}{dt}(\frac{dy}{dx}) = \frac{6t}{2t} - \frac{3t^2 - 1}{2t^2} = 3 - \frac{1}{t}$. Then, we divide this by $x'$ to get $\frac{d^2y}{dx^2} = \frac{3 - \frac{1}{t}}{2t} = \frac{3t - 1}{2t^2}$.

The second derivative tells us about the concavity of the curve defined by the parametric equations. If $\frac{d^2y}{dx^2} > 0$, the curve is concave up. If $\frac{d^2y}{dx^2} < 0$, the curve is concave down. If $\frac{d^2y}{dx^2} = 0$, the curve may have an inflection point.

In the next section, we will explore polar coordinates and how they provide an alternative way to represent points in the plane.

### Section: Parametric Equations

#### Subsection: Finding arc lengths of curves given by parametric equations

After understanding the concept of derivatives of parametric equations, let's move on to another important topic - finding the arc lengths of curves given by parametric equations. The arc length of a curve is the distance you would travel if you went along the curve from one point to another.

To find the arc length of a curve defined by parametric equations, we use the following formula:

$$
L = \int_a^b \sqrt{{dx/dt}^2 + {dy/dt}^2} dt
$$

where $L$ is the arc length, $a$ and $b$ are the limits of the parameter $t$, and $dx/dt$ and $dy/dt$ are the derivatives of the parametric equations with respect to $t$.

Let's illustrate this with an example. Consider the parametric equations $x = t^2$ and $y = t^3 - t$. The first derivatives are $x' = 2t$ and $y' = 3t^2 - 1$.

To find the arc length, we substitute these derivatives into the arc length formula:

$$
L = \int_a^b \sqrt{{(2t)}^2 + {(3t^2 - 1)}^2} dt
$$

This integral can be evaluated using standard techniques of integration. The result will give us the arc length of the curve between the points corresponding to $t = a$ and $t = b$.

Remember, the arc length gives us a measure of the "distance" along the curve, not the straight-line distance between the start and end points. This concept is particularly useful in physics and engineering, where we often need to calculate the length of a path followed by a particle or an object.

In the next section, we will continue our exploration of parametric equations by discussing polar coordinates and how they provide an alternative way to represent points in the plane.

### Section: Vector-Valued Functions

#### Subsection: Defining and differentiating vector-valued functions

Vector-valued functions are a significant concept in calculus, particularly in the study of motion and forces in physics. They are functions that take one or more real numbers as input and produce a vector as output.

A vector-valued function in two dimensions can be written as:

$$
\vec{r}(t) = \langle f(t), g(t) \rangle
$$

where $f(t)$ and $g(t)$ are real-valued functions of $t$. The angle brackets denote a vector whose components are given by the functions inside.

In three dimensions, a vector-valued function is written as:

$$
\vec{r}(t) = \langle f(t), g(t), h(t) \rangle
$$

where $f(t)$, $g(t)$, and $h(t)$ are real-valued functions of $t$.

The vector $\vec{r}(t)$ traces out a curve in two or three dimensions as $t$ varies. This curve is called the path or trajectory of the vector-valued function.

Now, let's discuss how to differentiate a vector-valued function. The derivative of a vector-valued function is found by differentiating each component function separately. If $\vec{r}(t) = \langle f(t), g(t) \rangle$, then the derivative of $\vec{r}$ with respect to $t$ is given by:

$$
\vec{r}'(t) = \langle f'(t), g'(t) \rangle
$$

Similarly, in three dimensions, if $\vec{r}(t) = \langle f(t), g(t), h(t) \rangle$, then the derivative of $\vec{r}$ with respect to $t$ is:

$$
\vec{r}'(t) = \langle f'(t), g'(t), h'(t) \rangle
$$

The derivative of a vector-valued function gives us a new vector-valued function that describes the rate of change of the original function. This is analogous to how the derivative of a real-valued function gives us the rate of change of that function.

In the next subsection, we will explore some applications of vector-valued functions and their derivatives, including the calculation of tangent vectors and the description of motion in physics.

#### Subsection: Solving motion problems using parametric and vector-valued functions

In the previous subsection, we introduced vector-valued functions and their derivatives. Now, we will apply these concepts to solve motion problems.

In physics, motion is described in terms of displacement, velocity, and acceleration, all of which can be represented as vector-valued functions.

##### Displacement

The displacement of an object moving in space is given by the vector-valued function $\vec{r}(t)$, which we have already discussed. The function $\vec{r}(t)$ gives the position of the object at any time $t$.

##### Velocity

The velocity of the object is given by the derivative of the displacement function, $\vec{r}'(t)$. This is because velocity is the rate of change of displacement. In two dimensions, if $\vec{r}(t) = \langle f(t), g(t) \rangle$, then the velocity function $\vec{v}(t)$ is given by:

$$
\vec{v}(t) = \vec{r}'(t) = \langle f'(t), g'(t) \rangle
$$

Similarly, in three dimensions, if $\vec{r}(t) = \langle f(t), g(t), h(t) \rangle$, then the velocity function $\vec{v}(t)$ is:

$$
\vec{v}(t) = \vec{r}'(t) = \langle f'(t), g'(t), h'(t) \rangle
$$

##### Acceleration

Acceleration is the rate of change of velocity, so the acceleration function $\vec{a}(t)$ is the derivative of the velocity function. In two dimensions, if $\vec{v}(t) = \langle f'(t), g'(t) \rangle$, then the acceleration function $\vec{a}(t)$ is given by:

$$
\vec{a}(t) = \vec{v}'(t) = \langle f''(t), g''(t) \rangle
$$

Similarly, in three dimensions, if $\vec{v}(t) = \langle f'(t), g'(t), h'(t) \rangle$, then the acceleration function $\vec{a}(t)$ is:

$$
\vec{a}(t) = \vec{v}'(t) = \langle f''(t), g''(t), h''(t) \rangle
$$

By using these vector-valued functions, we can describe the motion of an object in two or three dimensions. In the next subsection, we will look at some examples of how to apply these concepts to solve problems in physics.

### Section: Polar Coordinates

Polar coordinates offer an alternative way of representing points in the plane. Unlike Cartesian coordinates, which locate a point in the plane using a pair of distances from two perpendicular lines (the x and y axes), polar coordinates locate a point using the distance from a fixed point (the pole, usually denoted as the origin O) and the angle from a fixed direction (the polar axis, usually the positive x-axis).

#### Subsection: Defining polar coordinates

A point P in the plane can be represented in polar coordinates as $(r, \theta)$, where $r$ is the distance from P to the origin O, and $\theta$ is the angle measured counterclockwise from the polar axis to the line segment OP.

The polar coordinates of a point are not unique. For example, the point P can also be represented as $(r, \theta + 2\pi)$, since adding $2\pi$ to the angle does not change the direction of the line segment OP. Similarly, the point P can be represented as $(-r, \theta + \pi)$, since replacing $r$ with $-r$ and adding $\pi$ to the angle rotates the line segment OP by 180 degrees.

#### Subsection: Differentiating in polar form

When dealing with functions defined in terms of polar coordinates, we often need to compute derivatives. The process is similar to differentiation in Cartesian coordinates, but with some additional considerations due to the nature of polar coordinates.

If a function $f(r, \theta)$ is given in polar coordinates, the partial derivatives with respect to $r$ and $\theta$ are defined as follows:

$$
\frac{\partial f}{\partial r} = \lim_{\Delta r \to 0} \frac{f(r + \Delta r, \theta) - f(r, \theta)}{\Delta r}
$$

and

$$
\frac{\partial f}{\partial \theta} = \lim_{\Delta \theta \to 0} \frac{f(r, \theta + \Delta \theta) - f(r, \theta)}{\Delta \theta}
$$

These definitions are analogous to the definitions of the partial derivatives with respect to $x$ and $y$ in Cartesian coordinates.

In the next section, we will explore how to convert between Cartesian and polar coordinates, and how to integrate and differentiate functions in polar form.

#### Subsection: Finding the area of a polar region or the area bounded by a single polar curve

In this subsection, we will explore how to find the area of a region defined by a polar curve. This is a common problem in calculus and physics, and understanding how to solve it is crucial for mastering the subject.

The area $A$ of a sector of a circle with radius $r$ and central angle $\theta$ (in radians) is given by the formula $A = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$. This formula can be extended to find the area enclosed by a polar curve.

Suppose we have a polar curve defined by the equation $r = f(\theta)$, where $f$ is a continuous function and $\theta$ is in the interval $[\alpha, \beta]$. The area $A$ enclosed by the curve and the lines $\theta = \alpha$ and $\theta = \beta$ is given by the formula:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2}\int_{\alpha}^{\beta} [f(\theta)]^2 d\theta
$$

This formula is derived from the basic formula for the area of a sector of a circle, with the understanding that the polar curve can be approximated by a series of very small sectors.

Let's consider an example. Suppose we have the polar curve defined by $r = 2\cos(\theta)$, and we want to find the area enclosed by the curve and the lines $\theta = 0$ and $\theta = \pi/2$. We can use the formula above to find the area:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2}\int_{0}^{\pi/2} [2\cos(\theta)]^2 d\theta = 2\int_{0}^{\pi/2} \cos^2(\theta) d\theta
$$

Using the double-angle formula for cosine, we can simplify the integral and find the area.

In the next section, we will explore how to find the length of a polar curve.

#### Subsection: Finding the area of the region bounded by two polar curves

In the previous section, we learned how to find the area enclosed by a single polar curve. Now, let's extend this concept to find the area of the region bounded by two polar curves.

Suppose we have two polar curves defined by the equations $r = f(\theta)$ and $r = g(\theta)$, where $f$ and $g$ are continuous functions and $\theta$ is in the interval $[\alpha, \beta]$. If $f(\theta) \geq g(\theta)$ for all $\theta$ in $[\alpha, \beta]$, the area $A$ of the region bounded by the two curves and the lines $\theta = \alpha$ and $\theta = \beta$ is given by the formula:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2}\int_{\alpha}^{\beta} ([f(\theta)]^2 - [g(\theta)]^2) d\theta
$$

This formula is derived from the formula for the area enclosed by a single polar curve, with the understanding that the region between the two curves can be approximated by a series of very small annular sectors (sectors of annuli).

Let's consider an example. Suppose we have the polar curves defined by $r = 2\cos(\theta)$ and $r = \cos(\theta)$, and we want to find the area enclosed by the two curves and the lines $\theta = 0$ and $\theta = \pi/2$. We can use the formula above to find the area:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2}\int_{0}^{\pi/2} ([2\cos(\theta)]^2 - [\cos(\theta)]^2) d\theta = \frac{1}{2}\int_{0}^{\pi/2} (4\cos^2(\theta) - \cos^2(\theta)) d\theta = \frac{3}{2}\int_{0}^{\pi/2} \cos^2(\theta) d\theta
$$

Again, using the double-angle formula for cosine, we can simplify the integral and find the area.

In the next section, we will explore how to find the intersection points of two polar curves.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of parametric equations, polar coordinates, and vector-valued functions. We have seen how these mathematical concepts allow us to describe complex phenomena in a more intuitive and efficient way.

Parametric equations, for instance, have allowed us to express the relationship between two variables without explicitly defining one in terms of the other. This has given us the flexibility to model a wide range of real-world situations, from the trajectory of a projectile to the growth of a population.

Polar coordinates, on the other hand, have provided us with a different perspective on the plane. Instead of describing points in terms of their horizontal and vertical distances from the origin, we have learned to describe them in terms of their distance from the origin and the angle they make with the positive x-axis. This has proven particularly useful in situations where circular or rotational symmetry is present.

Finally, vector-valued functions have allowed us to deal with quantities that have both magnitude and direction. We have seen how these functions can be used to describe physical quantities such as velocity and force, as well as more abstract concepts like the position of a point in space.

In the next chapter, we will build on these concepts and explore their applications in calculus. We will learn how to differentiate and integrate parametric, polar, and vector-valued functions, and how these techniques can be used to solve a wide range of problems.

This has given us the flexibility to model a wide range of real-world situations, from the trajectory of a projectile to the growth of a population.

Polar coordinates, on the other hand, have provided us with a different perspective on the plane. Instead of describing points in terms of their horizontal and vertical distances from the origin, we have learned to describe them in terms of their distance from the origin and the angle they make with the positive x-axis. This has proven particularly useful in situations where circular or rotational symmetry is present.
